<p><b>New Hampshire</b> (<span class="nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">US</a></small> <span class="noexcerpt"><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7a/En-us-New_Hampshire.ogg" title="Listen"><img alt="Listen" data-file-height="11" data-file-width="11" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/11px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/17px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/22px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a><sup><span class="IPA" style="color:#00e;font:bold 80% sans-serif;padding:0 .1em"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-New_Hampshire.ogg" title="File:En-us-New Hampshire.ogg">i</a></span></sup></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="/uː/ long 'oo' in 'food'">uː</span></span> <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="/ʃ/ 'sh' in 'shy'">ʃ</span><span title="/ər/ 'er' in 'finger'">ər</span></span>/</a></span></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">state</a> in the <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> region of the <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">northeastern</a> <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. It is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> to the south, <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a> to the west, <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> to the east, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_province" title="Canadian province">Canadian province</a> of <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a> to the north. New Hampshire is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_area" title="List of U.S. states and territories by area">5th smallest</a> by land area and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_population" title="List of U.S. states and territories by population">9th least populous</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states" title="List of U.S. states">50 United States</a>.</p>
<p>In January 1776, it became the first of the British North American <a href="/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="European colonization of the Americas">colonies</a> to establish a government independent of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a>'s authority, and it was the first to establish its own state constitution. Six months later, it became one of the original <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">13 states</a> that <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">founded</a> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_of_America" title="United States of America">United States of America</a>, and in June 1788 it was the ninth state to ratify the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">Constitution</a>, bringing that document into effect.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Concord,_New_Hampshire" title="Concord, New Hampshire">Concord</a> is the state capital, while <a href="/wiki/Manchester,_New_Hampshire" title="Manchester, New Hampshire">Manchester</a> is the largest city in the state and in northern New England, including Vermont and Maine. It has no general <a href="/wiki/Sales_taxes_in_the_United_States" title="Sales taxes in the United States">sales tax</a>, nor is personal income (other than interest and dividends) <a href="/wiki/Income_tax" title="Income tax">taxed</a> at either the state or local level. The <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_primary" title="New Hampshire primary">New Hampshire primary</a> is the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_primary" title="U.S. presidential primary">primary</a> in the U.S. presidential election cycle. Its license plates carry the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._state_mottos" title="List of U.S. state mottos">state motto</a>, "<a href="/wiki/Live_Free_or_Die" title="Live Free or Die">Live Free or Die</a>". The state's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_nickname" title="State nickname">nickname</a>, "The <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">Granite</a> State", refers to its extensive granite formations and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quarries" title="Quarries">quarries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among <a href="/wiki/List_of_people_from_New_Hampshire" title="List of people from New Hampshire">prominent individuals from New Hampshire</a> are <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">founding father</a> <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Gilman" title="Nicholas Gilman">Nicholas Gilman</a>, Senator <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Webster" title="Daniel Webster">Daniel Webster</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">Revolutionary War</a> hero <a href="/wiki/John_Stark" title="John Stark">John Stark</a>, editor <a href="/wiki/Horace_Greeley" title="Horace Greeley">Horace Greeley</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Science" title="Christian Science">Christian Science</a> religion <a href="/wiki/Mary_Baker_Eddy" title="Mary Baker Eddy">Mary Baker Eddy</a>, poet <a href="/wiki/Robert_Frost" title="Robert Frost">Robert Frost</a>, astronaut <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shepard" title="Alan Shepard">Alan Shepard</a>, rock musician <a href="/wiki/Ronnie_James_Dio" title="Ronnie James Dio">Ronnie James Dio</a>, author <a href="/wiki/Dan_Brown" title="Dan Brown">Dan Brown</a>, actor <a href="/wiki/Adam_Sandler" title="Adam Sandler">Adam Sandler</a>, inventor <a href="/wiki/Dean_Kamen" title="Dean Kamen">Dean Kamen</a>, comedian <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Silverman" title="Sarah Silverman">Sarah Silverman</a>, restaurateurs <a href="/wiki/Richard_and_Maurice_McDonald" title="Richard and Maurice McDonald">Richard and Maurice McDonald</a>, and President of the United States <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce" title="Franklin Pierce">Franklin Pierce</a>.</p>
<p>With some of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_New_England_ski_areas_by_vertical_drop" title="List of New England ski areas by vertical drop">largest ski mountains on the East Coast</a>, New Hampshire's major recreational attractions include <a href="/wiki/Skiing" title="Skiing">skiing</a>, snowmobiling, and other winter sports, hiking and mountaineering, observing the fall foliage, summer cottages along many lakes and the seacoast, motor sports at the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_Motor_Speedway" title="New Hampshire Motor Speedway">New Hampshire Motor Speedway</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Laconia_Motorcycle_Week" title="Laconia Motorcycle Week">Motorcycle Week</a>, a popular motorcycle rally held in <a href="/wiki/Weirs_Beach,_New_Hampshire" title="Weirs Beach, New Hampshire">Weirs Beach</a> near <a href="/wiki/Laconia,_New_Hampshire" title="Laconia, New Hampshire">Laconia</a> in June. The <a href="/wiki/White_Mountain_National_Forest" title="White Mountain National Forest">White Mountain National Forest</a> links the Vermont and Maine portions of the <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Trail" title="Appalachian Trail">Appalachian Trail</a>, and boasts the <a href="/wiki/Mount_Washington_Auto_Road" title="Mount Washington Auto Road">Mount Washington Auto Road</a>, where visitors may drive to the top of 6,288-foot (1,917 m) <a href="/wiki/Mount_Washington_(New_Hampshire)" title="Mount Washington (New Hampshire)">Mount Washington</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Hampshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The state was named after the southern <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">English</a> <a href="/wiki/Counties_of_England" title="Counties of England">county</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hampshire" title="Hampshire">Hampshire</a> by <a href="/wiki/John_Mason_(governor)" title="John Mason (governor)">Captain John Mason</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Hampshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:National-atlas-new-hampshire.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="587" data-file-width="764" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/National-atlas-new-hampshire.png/220px-National-atlas-new-hampshire.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/National-atlas-new-hampshire.png/330px-National-atlas-new-hampshire.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/National-atlas-new-hampshire.png/440px-National-atlas-new-hampshire.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:National-atlas-new-hampshire.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
New Hampshire, showing roads, rivers and major cities</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_Hampshire_Shaded_Relief_5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="389" data-file-width="224" height="382" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/New_Hampshire_Shaded_Relief_5.jpg/220px-New_Hampshire_Shaded_Relief_5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/88/New_Hampshire_Shaded_Relief_5.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mount_Adams_NH_from_Madison.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Mount_Adams_NH_from_Madison.jpg/220px-Mount_Adams_NH_from_Madison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Mount_Adams_NH_from_Madison.jpg/330px-Mount_Adams_NH_from_Madison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Mount_Adams_NH_from_Madison.jpg/440px-Mount_Adams_NH_from_Madison.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

Mount Adams (5,774 ft or 1,760 m) is part of New Hampshire's <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Range" title="Presidential Range">Presidential Range</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lake_Winnipesaukee_and_the_Ossipee_Mountains.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1702" data-file-width="2560" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Lake_Winnipesaukee_and_the_Ossipee_Mountains.jpg/220px-Lake_Winnipesaukee_and_the_Ossipee_Mountains.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Lake_Winnipesaukee_and_the_Ossipee_Mountains.jpg/330px-Lake_Winnipesaukee_and_the_Ossipee_Mountains.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Lake_Winnipesaukee_and_the_Ossipee_Mountains.jpg/440px-Lake_Winnipesaukee_and_the_Ossipee_Mountains.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

<a href="/wiki/Lake_Winnipesaukee" title="Lake Winnipesaukee">Lake Winnipesaukee</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ossipee_Mountains" title="Ossipee Mountains">Ossipee Mountains</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_counties_in_New_Hampshire" title="List of counties in New Hampshire">List of counties in New Hampshire</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_mountains_in_New_Hampshire" title="List of mountains in New Hampshire">List of mountains in New Hampshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_lakes_in_New_Hampshire" title="List of lakes in New Hampshire">List of lakes in New Hampshire</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_in_New_Hampshire" title="List of rivers in New Hampshire">List of rivers in New Hampshire</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_New_Hampshire" title="Geology of New Hampshire">Geology of New Hampshire</a></div>
<p>New Hampshire is part of the <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> region. It is bounded by <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, Canada, to the north and northwest; <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a> and the Atlantic Ocean to the east; <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> to the south; and <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a> to the west. New Hampshire's major regions are the <a href="/wiki/Great_North_Woods_Region_(New_Hampshire)" title="Great North Woods Region (New Hampshire)">Great North Woods</a>, the <a href="/wiki/White_Mountains_Region" title="White Mountains Region">White Mountains</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lakes_Region_(New_Hampshire)" title="Lakes Region (New Hampshire)">Lakes Region</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Seacoast_Region_(New_Hampshire)" title="Seacoast Region (New Hampshire)">Seacoast</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Merrimack_Valley" title="Merrimack Valley">Merrimack Valley</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Monadnock_Region" title="Monadnock Region">Monadnock Region</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dartmouth-Lake_Sunapee_Region" title="Dartmouth-Lake Sunapee Region">Dartmouth-Lake Sunapee</a> area. New Hampshire has the shortest ocean coastline of any U.S. coastal state, with a length of 18 miles (29 km),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> sometimes measured as only 13 miles (21 km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> New Hampshire was home to the rock formation called the <a href="/wiki/Old_Man_of_the_Mountain" title="Old Man of the Mountain">Old Man of the Mountain</a>, a face-like profile in <a href="/wiki/Franconia_Notch" title="Franconia Notch">Franconia Notch</a>, until the formation disintegrated in May 2003.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/White_Mountains_(New_Hampshire)" title="White Mountains (New Hampshire)">White Mountains range</a> in New Hampshire spans the north-central portion of the state, with <a href="/wiki/Mount_Washington_(New_Hampshire)" title="Mount Washington (New Hampshire)">Mount Washington</a> the tallest in the northeastern U.S. – site of the second-highest wind speed ever recorded<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> – and other mountains like <a href="/wiki/Mount_Madison" title="Mount Madison">Mount Madison</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Adams_(New_Hampshire)" title="Mount Adams (New Hampshire)">Mount Adams</a> surrounding it. With hurricane-force winds every third day on average, over 100 recorded deaths among visitors, and conspicuous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Krumholtz" title="Krumholtz">krumholtz</a> (dwarf, matted trees much like a carpet of <a href="/wiki/Bonsai" title="Bonsai">bonsai</a> trees), the climate on the upper reaches of Mount Washington has inspired the weather observatory on the peak to claim that the area has the "World's Worst Weather".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the flatter southwest corner of New Hampshire, the landmark <a href="/wiki/Mount_Monadnock" title="Mount Monadnock">Mount Monadnock</a> has given its name to a class of earth-forms – a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monadnock" title="Monadnock">monadnock</a></i> – signifying, in geomorphology, any isolated resistant peak rising from a less resistant eroded plain.</p>
<p>Major rivers include the 110-mile (177 km) <a href="/wiki/Merrimack_River" title="Merrimack River">Merrimack River</a>, which bisects the lower half of the state north–south and ends up in <a href="/wiki/Newburyport,_Massachusetts" title="Newburyport, Massachusetts">Newburyport, Massachusetts</a>. Its tributaries include the <a href="/wiki/Contoocook_River" title="Contoocook River">Contoocook River</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pemigewasset_River" title="Pemigewasset River">Pemigewasset River</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Winnipesaukee_River" title="Winnipesaukee River">Winnipesaukee River</a>. The 410-mile (660 km) <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_River" title="Connecticut River">Connecticut River</a>, which starts at New Hampshire's <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Lakes" title="Connecticut Lakes">Connecticut Lakes</a> and flows south to <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>, defines the western border with Vermont. The state border is not in the center of that river, as is usually the case, but at the low-water mark on the Vermont side; meaning that the entire river along the Vermont border (save for areas where the water level has been raised by a dam) lies within New Hampshire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Only one town – <a href="/wiki/Pittsburg,_New_Hampshire" title="Pittsburg, New Hampshire">Pittsburg</a> – shares a land border with the state of Vermont. The "northwesternmost headwaters" of the Connecticut also define the Canada–US border.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Piscataqua_River" title="Piscataqua River">Piscataqua River</a> and its several <a href="/wiki/Tributary" title="Tributary">tributaries</a> form the state's only significant ocean port where they flow into the Atlantic at <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth,_New_Hampshire" title="Portsmouth, New Hampshire">Portsmouth</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Salmon_Falls_River" title="Salmon Falls River">Salmon Falls River</a> and the Piscataqua define the southern portion of the border with Maine. The Piscataqua River boundary was the subject of a <a href="/wiki/Piscataqua_River_border_dispute" title="Piscataqua River border dispute">border dispute</a> between New Hampshire and Maine in 2001, with New Hampshire claiming dominion over several islands (primarily <a href="/wiki/Seavey%27s_Island" title="Seavey's Island">Seavey's Island</a>) that include the <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth_Naval_Shipyard" title="Portsmouth Naval Shipyard">Portsmouth Naval Shipyard</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court" title="United States Supreme Court">U.S. Supreme Court</a> dismissed the case in 2002, leaving ownership of the island with Maine. New Hampshire still claims sovereignty of the base, however.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>The largest of <a href="/wiki/List_of_lakes_in_New_Hampshire" title="List of lakes in New Hampshire">New Hampshire's lakes</a> is <a href="/wiki/Lake_Winnipesaukee" title="Lake Winnipesaukee">Lake Winnipesaukee</a>, which covers 71 square miles (184 km<sup>2</sup>) in the east-central part of New Hampshire. <a href="/wiki/Umbagog_Lake" title="Umbagog Lake">Umbagog Lake</a> along the Maine border, approximately 12.3 square miles (31.9 km<sup>2</sup>), is a distant second. <a href="/wiki/Squam_Lake" title="Squam Lake">Squam Lake</a> is the second largest lake entirely in New Hampshire.</p>
<p>New Hampshire has the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_by_coastline" title="List of U.S. states by coastline">shortest ocean coastline</a> of any state in the United States, approximately 18 miles (29 km) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hampton_Beach,_New_Hampshire" title="Hampton Beach, New Hampshire">Hampton Beach</a> is a popular local summer destination. About 7 miles (11 km) offshore are the <a href="/wiki/Isles_of_Shoals" title="Isles of Shoals">Isles of Shoals</a>, nine small islands (four of which are in New Hampshire) known as the site of a 19th-century art colony founded by poet <a href="/wiki/Celia_Thaxter" title="Celia Thaxter">Celia Thaxter</a>, and the alleged location of one of the buried treasures of the pirate <a href="/wiki/Blackbeard" title="Blackbeard">Blackbeard</a>.</p>
<p>It is the state with the highest percentage of timberland area in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> New Hampshire is in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate_broadleaf_and_mixed_forests" title="Temperate broadleaf and mixed forests">temperate broadleaf and mixed forests</a> <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">biome</a>. Much of the state, in particular the White Mountains, is covered by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conifer" title="Conifer">conifers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_hardwood_forest" title="Northern hardwood forest">northern hardwoods</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_England-Acadian_forests" title="New England-Acadian forests">New England-Acadian forests</a>. The southeast corner of the state and parts of the Connecticut River along the Vermont border are covered by the mixed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quercus" title="Quercus">oaks</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_coastal_forests" title="Northeastern coastal forests">Northeastern coastal forests</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ecoregions_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-ecoregions-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>The northern third of the state is locally referred to as the "north country" or "north of the notches," in reference to White Mountain <a href="/wiki/Mountain_pass" title="Mountain pass">passes</a> that channel traffic. It contains less than 5% of the state's population, suffers relatively high poverty, and is steadily losing population as the logging and paper industries decline. However, the tourist industry, in particular visitors who go to northern New Hampshire to <a href="/wiki/Skiing" title="Skiing">ski</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snowboarding" title="Snowboarding">snowboard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hiking" title="Hiking">hike</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mountain_biking" title="Mountain biking">mountain bike</a>, has helped offset economic losses from mill closures.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Hampshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NH_koppen.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1710" data-file-width="900" height="418" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/NH_koppen.svg/220px-NH_koppen.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/NH_koppen.svg/330px-NH_koppen.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/NH_koppen.svg/440px-NH_koppen.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NH_koppen.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Köppen climate types in New Hampshire</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_hampshire_in_autumn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/New_hampshire_in_autumn.jpg/220px-New_hampshire_in_autumn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/New_hampshire_in_autumn.jpg/330px-New_hampshire_in_autumn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/New_hampshire_in_autumn.jpg/440px-New_hampshire_in_autumn.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_hampshire_in_autumn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
During autumn, the leaves on many hardwood trees in New Hampshire turn colors, attracting many tourists.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Winter_in_New_Hampshire.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Winter_in_New_Hampshire.JPG/220px-Winter_in_New_Hampshire.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Winter_in_New_Hampshire.JPG/330px-Winter_in_New_Hampshire.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Winter_in_New_Hampshire.JPG/440px-Winter_in_New_Hampshire.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Winter_in_New_Hampshire.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A winter view of an apple orchard in Hollis, New Hampshire</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>New Hampshire experiences a <a href="/wiki/Humid_continental_climate" title="Humid continental climate">humid continental climate</a> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a> <i>Dfa</i> in southern areas and <i>Dfb</i> in the north), with warm, humid summers, cold, wet winters, and uniform precipitation all year. The climate of the southeastern portion is moderated by the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> and averages relatively milder and wetter weather, while the northern and interior portions experience cooler temperatures and lower humidity. Winters are cold and snowy throughout the state, and especially severe in the northern and mountainous areas. Average annual snowfall ranges from 60 inches (150 cm) to over 100 inches (250 cm) across the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaasnow_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-noaasnow-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Average daytime highs are in the mid 70s°F to low 80s°F (around 24–28 °C) throughout the state in July, with overnight lows in the mid 50s°F to low 60s°F (13–15 °C). January temperatures range from an average high of 34 °F (1 °C) on the coast to overnight lows below 0 °F (−18 °C) in the far north and at high elevations. Average annual precipitation statewide is roughly 40 inches (100 cm) with some variation occurring in the <a href="/wiki/White_Mountains_(New_Hampshire)" title="White Mountains (New Hampshire)">White Mountains</a> due to differences in elevation and annual snowfall. New Hampshire's highest recorded temperature was 106 °F (41 °C) in <a href="/wiki/Nashua,_New_Hampshire" title="Nashua, New Hampshire">Nashua</a> on July 4, 1911, while the lowest recorded temperature was −47 °F (−44 °C) atop <a href="/wiki/Mount_Washington_(New_Hampshire)" title="Mount Washington (New Hampshire)">Mount Washington</a> on January 29, 1934. Mount Washington also saw an unofficial −50 °F (−46 °C) reading on January 22, 1885, which, if made official, would tie the all-time record low for New England (also −50 °F (−46 °C) at <a href="/wiki/Big_Black_River_(Saint_John_River)" title="Big Black River (Saint John River)">Big Black River</a>, Maine, on January 16, 2009, and <a href="/wiki/Bloomfield,_Vermont" title="Bloomfield, Vermont">Bloomfield, Vermont</a> on December 30, 1933).</p>
<p>Extreme snow is often associated with a <a href="/wiki/Nor%27easter" title="Nor'easter">nor'easter</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States_blizzard_of_1978" title="Northeastern United States blizzard of 1978">Blizzard of '78</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blizzard_of_1993" title="Blizzard of 1993">Blizzard of 1993</a>, when several feet accumulated across portions of the state over 24 to 48 hours. Lighter snowfalls of several inches occur frequently throughout winter, often associated with an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alberta_Clipper" title="Alberta Clipper">Alberta Clipper</a>.</p>
<p>New Hampshire, on occasion, is affected by <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">hurricanes</a> and tropical storms although by the time they reach the state they are often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extratropical" title="Extratropical">extratropical</a>, with most storms striking the southern <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> coastline and moving inland or passing by offshore in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Maine" title="Gulf of Maine">Gulf of Maine</a>. Most of New Hampshire averages fewer than 20 days of thunderstorms per year and an average of two tornadoes occur annually statewide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaatornado_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-noaatornado-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Arbor_Day_Foundation" title="National Arbor Day Foundation">National Arbor Day Foundation</a> plant <a href="/wiki/Hardiness_zone" title="Hardiness zone">hardiness zone</a> map depicts zones 3, 4, 5, and 6 occurring throughout the state<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arborday_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-arborday-26">[26]</a></sup> and indicates the transition from a relatively cooler to warmer climate as one travels southward across New Hampshire. The 1990 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USDA" title="USDA">USDA</a> plant <a href="/wiki/Hardiness_zone" title="Hardiness zone">hardiness zones</a> for New Hampshire range from zone 3b in the north to zone 5b in the south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metropolitan_areas">Metropolitan areas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Hampshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Metropolitan areas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">

</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_New_Hampshire" title="List of cities in New Hampshire">List of cities in New Hampshire</a></div>

The following is a list of NECTAs in New Hampshire:</p>

<div style="clear:right;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Hampshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_New_Hampshire" title="History of New Hampshire">History of New Hampshire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Detail_of_Fort_William_and_Mary,_1705.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="313" data-file-width="350" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Detail_of_Fort_William_and_Mary%2C_1705.jpg/220px-Detail_of_Fort_William_and_Mary%2C_1705.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Detail_of_Fort_William_and_Mary%2C_1705.jpg/330px-Detail_of_Fort_William_and_Mary%2C_1705.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5f/Detail_of_Fort_William_and_Mary%2C_1705.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Detail_of_Fort_William_and_Mary,_1705.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Fort_William_and_Mary" title="Fort William and Mary">Fort William and Mary</a> in 1705</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:State_of_New_Hampshire.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="656" data-file-width="543" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d6/State_of_New_Hampshire.JPG/220px-State_of_New_Hampshire.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d6/State_of_New_Hampshire.JPG/330px-State_of_New_Hampshire.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d6/State_of_New_Hampshire.JPG/440px-State_of_New_Hampshire.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:State_of_New_Hampshire.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1922 map of New Hampshire published in the bulletin of the <a href="/wiki/Brown_Company" title="Brown Company">Brown Company</a> in <a href="/wiki/Berlin,_New_Hampshire" title="Berlin, New Hampshire">Berlin</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Various <a href="/wiki/Algonquian_peoples" title="Algonquian peoples">Algonquian</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abenaki_people" title="Abenaki people">Abenaki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pennacook" title="Pennacook">Pennacook</a>) tribes inhabited the area before European settlement. English and French explorers visited New Hampshire in 1600–1605, and English fishermen settled at <a href="/wiki/Odiorne_Point_State_Park" title="Odiorne Point State Park">Odiorne's Point</a> in present-day <a href="/wiki/Rye,_New_Hampshire" title="Rye, New Hampshire">Rye</a> in 1623. The first permanent settlement was at Hilton's Point (present-day <a href="/wiki/Dover,_New_Hampshire" title="Dover, New Hampshire">Dover</a>). By 1631, the Upper Plantation comprised modern-day <a href="/wiki/Dover,_New_Hampshire" title="Dover, New Hampshire">Dover</a>, <a href="/wiki/Durham,_New_Hampshire" title="Durham, New Hampshire">Durham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stratham,_New_Hampshire" title="Stratham, New Hampshire">Stratham</a>; in 1679, it became the "Royal Province". <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Father_Rale%27s_War" title="Father Rale's War">Father Rale's War</a> was fought between the colonists and the <a href="/wiki/Wabanaki_Confederacy" title="Wabanaki Confederacy">Wabanaki Confederacy</a> throughout New Hampshire.</p>
<p>New Hampshire was one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thirteen_colonies" title="Thirteen colonies">thirteen colonies</a> that rebelled against British rule during the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>. By the time of the American Revolution, New Hampshire was a divided province. The economic and social life of the Seacoast region revolved around sawmills, shipyards, merchants' warehouses, and established village and town centers. Wealthy merchants built substantial homes, furnished them with the finest luxuries, and invested their capital in trade and land speculation. At the other end of the social scale, there developed a permanent class of day laborers, mariners, indentured servants and even slaves.</p>
<p>The only battle fought in New Hampshire was the raid on <a href="/wiki/Fort_William_and_Mary" title="Fort William and Mary">Fort William and Mary</a>, December 14, 1774, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portsmouth_Harbor" title="Portsmouth Harbor">Portsmouth Harbor</a>, which netted the rebellion sizable quantities of gunpowder, small arms and cannon. (<a href="/wiki/John_Sullivan_(general)" title="John Sullivan (general)">General Sullivan</a>, leader of the raid, described it as, "remainder of the powder, the small arms, bayonets, and cartouche-boxes, together with the cannon and ordnance stores") over the course of two nights. This raid was preceded by a warning to local patriots the previous day, by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Revere" title="Paul Revere">Paul Revere</a> on December 13, 1774, that the fort was to be reinforced by troops sailing from Boston. According to unverified accounts, the gunpowder was later used at the Battle of Bunker Hill, transported there by Major Demerit, who was one of several New Hampshire patriots who stored the powder in their homes until it was transported elsewhere for use in revolutionary activities. During the raid, the British soldiers fired upon the rebels with cannon and muskets. Although there were apparently no casualties, these were among the first shots in the American Revolutionary period, occurring approximately five months before the <a href="/wiki/Battles_of_Lexington_and_Concord" title="Battles of Lexington and Concord">Battles of Lexington and Concord</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a> was ratified by New Hampshire on June 21, 1788, when New Hampshire became the ninth state to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>New Hampshire was a Jacksonian stronghold; the state sent <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce" title="Franklin Pierce">Franklin Pierce</a> to the White House in the election of 1852. Industrialization took the form of numerous textile mills, which in turn attracted large flows of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immigrants" title="Immigrants">immigrants</a> from Quebec (the "French Canadians") and <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>. The northern parts of the state produced lumber, and the mountains provided tourist attractions. After 1960, the textile industry collapsed, but the economy rebounded as a center of high technology and as a service provider.</p>
<p>Starting in 1952, New Hampshire gained national and international attention for its <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_primary" title="New Hampshire primary">presidential primary</a> held early in every presidential election year. It immediately became the most important testing grounds for candidates for the Republican and Democratic nominations. The media gave New Hampshire (and <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>) about half of all the attention paid to all states in the primary process, magnifying the state's decision powers (and spurring repeated efforts by out-of-state politicians to change the rules.)</p>

New Hampshire population density</div>

<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">United States Census Bureau</a> estimates that the population of New Hampshire was 1,330,608 on July 1, 2015, a 1.07% increase since the <a href="/wiki/2010_United_States_Census" title="2010 United States Census">2010 United States Census</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopEstUS_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-PopEstUS-6">[6]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Center_of_population" title="Center of population">center of population</a> of New Hampshire is located in <a href="/wiki/Merrimack_County,_New_Hampshire" title="Merrimack County, New Hampshire">Merrimack County</a>, in the town of <a href="/wiki/Pembroke,_New_Hampshire" title="Pembroke, New Hampshire">Pembroke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The center of population has moved south 12 miles (19 km) since 1950,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> a reflection of the fact that the fastest growth in the state has been along its southern border, which is within commuting range of Boston and other Massachusetts cities.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Race_and_ancestry">Race and ancestry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Hampshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Race and ancestry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NH_ancestry.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="684" data-file-width="394" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/NH_ancestry.png/150px-NH_ancestry.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/NH_ancestry.png/225px-NH_ancestry.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/NH_ancestry.png/300px-NH_ancestry.png 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NH_ancestry.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Largest reported ancestry groups in New Hampshire by town. Dark purple indicates Irish, light purple English, turquoise French, pink French Canadian, dark blue Italian, and light blue German. Grey indicates towns(hips) with no reported population as of 2013.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to the 2010 U.S. Census, the racial makeup of New Hampshire was:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>93.9% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_American" title="White American">White American</a> (92.3% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-Hispanic_White" title="Non-Hispanic White">Non-Hispanic White</a>, 1.6% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_Hispanic" title="White Hispanic">White Hispanic</a>)</li>
<li>2.2% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_American" title="Asian American">Asian American</a></li>
<li>1.1% Black or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African American</a></li>
<li>0.2% <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American/American Indian</a></li>
<li>1.6% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiracial_American" title="Multiracial American">Two or more races</a></li>
<li>1.0% Some other race</li>
</ul>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_American" title="Hispanic and Latino American">Hispanic and Latino Americans</a> of any race made up 2.8% of the population in 2010.</p>
<table class="wikitable sortable collapsible" style="font-size: 90%;">
<caption><b>New Hampshire Racial Breakdown of Population</b></caption>
<tr>

<p>The largest ancestry groups in New Hampshire are, per 2013 Census Bureau estimates:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>23.3% <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Canadian" title="French Canadian">French Canadian</a></li>
<li>20.5% <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish</a></li>
<li>16.1% <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English</a></li>
<li>10.7% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_people" title="Italian people">Italian</a></li>
<li>8.3% <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">German</a></li>
<li>5.2% <a href="/wiki/Americans" title="Americans">American</a></li>
<li>4.7% <a href="/wiki/Poles" title="Poles">Polish</a></li>
<li>4.1% <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish</a></li>
<li>2.0% <a href="/wiki/Swedes" title="Swedes">Swedish</a></li>
<li>1.5% <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_people" title="Portuguese people">Portuguese</a></li>
<li>1.5% <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a></li>
<li>1.2% <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_people" title="Ulster Scots people">Scots-Irish</a></li>
<li>1.0% <a href="/wiki/Dutch_people" title="Dutch people">Dutch</a></li>
</ul>
<p>The large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irish_American" title="Irish American">Irish American</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French-Canadian" title="French-Canadian">French-Canadian</a> populations are descended largely from mill workers, and many still live in the former mill towns, like Manchester. New Hampshire has one of the highest percentages (23.3% of the population) of residents of French/French-Canadian/Acadian ancestry of any U.S. state. (As of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=New_Hampshire&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> estimates, Maine had a slightly higher percentage.)</p>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/2000_United_States_Census" title="2000 United States Census">2000 United States Census</a>, 3.4% of the population aged 5 and older speak <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> at home, while 1.6% speak <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MLA_Language_Map_Data_Center_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-MLA_Language_Map_Data_Center-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Co%C3%B6s_County,_New_Hampshire" title="Coös County, New Hampshire">Coös County</a>, 16% of the population speaks French at home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MLA_Language_Map_Data_Center_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-MLA_Language_Map_Data_Center-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Hampshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>A Pew survey showed that the religious affiliations of the people of New Hampshire was as follows: <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> 34%, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholicism_in_the_United_States" title="Catholicism in the United States">Catholic</a> 29%, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latter-day_Saint" title="Latter-day Saint">LDS</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mormon" title="Mormon">Mormon</a>) 1%, Jewish 1%, Jehovah's Witness 0.5%, Muslim 0.5%, Buddhist 1%, Hindu 0.5% and non-religious at 26%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>A survey suggests that people in New Hampshire and Vermont<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> are less likely than other Americans to attend weekly services and only 54% say that they are "absolutely certain there is a God" compared to 71% in the rest of the nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> New Hampshire and Vermont are also at the lowest levels among states in religious commitment. In 2012, 23% of New Hampshire residents in a Gallup poll considered themselves "very religious", while 52% considered themselves "non-religious".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Religion_Data_Archives" title="Association of Religion Data Archives">Association of Religion Data Archives</a> (ARDA) the largest denominations are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> with 311,028 members; The <a href="/wiki/United_Church_of_Christ" title="United Church of Christ">United Church of Christ</a> with 26,321 members; and the <a href="/wiki/United_Methodist_Church" title="United Methodist Church">United Methodist Church</a> with 18,029 members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.thearda.com_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-www.thearda.com-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Hampshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_locations_by_per_capita_income" title="New Hampshire locations by per capita income">New Hampshire locations by per capita income</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Economic_Analysis" title="Bureau of Economic Analysis">Bureau of Economic Analysis</a> estimates that New Hampshire's <a href="/wiki/Gross_state_product" title="Gross state product">total state product</a> in 2014 was $66 billion, ranking 40th in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Median_household_income" title="Median household income">Median household income</a> in 2008 was $49,467, the seventh highest in the country. Its agricultural outputs are dairy products, nursery stock, cattle, apples and eggs. Its industrial outputs are machinery, electric equipment, rubber and plastic products and tourism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>New Hampshire experienced a significant shift in its economic base during the last century. Historically, the base was composed of the traditional New England manufactures of textiles, shoe making, and small machining shops drawing upon low-wage labor from nearby small farms and from parts of Quebec. Today, these sectors contribute only 2% for textiles, 2% for leather goods, and 9% for machining of the state's total manufacturing dollar value (Source: U.S. Economic Census for 1997, Manufacturing, New Hampshire). They experienced a sharp decline due to obsolete plants and the lure of cheaper wages in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">South</a>.</p>
<p>The state's <a href="/wiki/Budget" title="Budget">budget</a> in FY2008 was $5.11 billion, including $1.48 billion in federal funds. The issue of taxation is controversial in New Hampshire, which has a <a href="/wiki/Property_tax" title="Property tax">property tax</a> (subject to municipal control) but no broad <a href="/wiki/Sales_tax" title="Sales tax">sales tax</a> or income tax. The state does have narrower taxes on meals, lodging, vehicles, business and investment income, and tolls on state roads.</p>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">Energy Information Administration</a>, New Hampshire's energy consumption and per capita energy consumption are among the lowest in the country. The <a href="/wiki/Seabrook_Station_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Seabrook Station Nuclear Power Plant">Seabrook Station Nuclear Power Plant</a>, located near Portsmouth, is the largest nuclear reactor in New England and provides about 30 percent of New Hampshire's electricity. Two natural gas-fired plants and some fossil-fuel powered plants, including the coal-fired Merrimack Station plant in Bow, provide most of the rest.</p>
<p>New Hampshire's residential electricity use is low compared with the national average, in part because demand for air conditioning is low during the generally mild summer months and because few households use electricity as their primary energy source for home heating. Over half of New Hampshire households use <a href="/wiki/Fuel_oil" title="Fuel oil">fuel oil</a> for winter heating. New Hampshire has potential for renewable energies like <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wood_fuel" title="Wood fuel">wood fuel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>The state has no general sales tax and no personal <a href="/wiki/State_income_tax" title="State income tax">state income tax</a> (the state does tax, at a 5 percent rate, income from dividends and interest), and the legislature has exercised fiscal restraint. Efforts to diversify the state's general economy have been ongoing.</p>
<p>New Hampshire's lack of a broad-based tax system has resulted in the state's local communities having some of the nation's highest property taxes. However, the state's overall tax burden is relatively low; in 2010 New Hampshire ranked 44th highest among states in combined average state and local tax burden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of February 2010, the state's unemployment rate was 7.1%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> By October 2010, the unemployment rate dropped to 5.4%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a 2013 study by Phoenix Marketing International, New Hampshire had the eighth-highest number of millionaires per capita in the United States, with a ratio of 6.48 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> In 2013, New Hampshire also had the nation's lowest poverty rate at just 8.7% of all residents according to the Census Bureau.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_and_government">Law and government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Hampshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Law and government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Concord_New_Hampshire_state_house_20041229.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1542" data-file-width="1976" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Concord_New_Hampshire_state_house_20041229.jpg/220px-Concord_New_Hampshire_state_house_20041229.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Concord_New_Hampshire_state_house_20041229.jpg/330px-Concord_New_Hampshire_state_house_20041229.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Concord_New_Hampshire_state_house_20041229.jpg/440px-Concord_New_Hampshire_state_house_20041229.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Concord_New_Hampshire_state_house_20041229.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_State_House" title="New Hampshire State House">New Hampshire State House</a> in <a href="/wiki/Concord,_New_Hampshire" title="Concord, New Hampshire">Concord</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Government_of_New_Hampshire" title="Government of New Hampshire">Government of New Hampshire</a></div>
<p>The governor of New Hampshire, as of January 5, 2017, is <a href="/wiki/Chris_Sununu" title="Chris Sununu">Chris Sununu</a> (Republican). New Hampshire's two U.S. senators are <a href="/wiki/Jeanne_Shaheen" title="Jeanne Shaheen">Jeanne Shaheen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maggie_Hassan" title="Maggie Hassan">Maggie Hassan</a> (both Democrats). New Hampshire's two U.S. representatives are <a href="/wiki/Carol_Shea-Porter" title="Carol Shea-Porter">Carol Shea-Porter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ann_McLane_Kuster" title="Ann McLane Kuster">Ann McLane Kuster</a> (both Democrats).</p>
<p>New Hampshire is an <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage_control_state" title="Alcoholic beverage control state">alcoholic beverage control state</a>, and through the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_Liquor_Commission" title="New Hampshire Liquor Commission">State Liquor Commission</a> it takes in $100 million from the sale and distribution of liquor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>New Hampshire is the only state in the US that does not require adults to wear seat belts in their vehicles. The state made <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_New_Hampshire" title="Same-sex marriage in New Hampshire">same-sex marriage</a> legal on January 1, 2010, just two years after it had made <a href="/wiki/Civil_union" title="Civil union">civil unions</a> legal.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Governing_documents">Governing documents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Hampshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Governing documents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_State_Constitution" title="New Hampshire State Constitution">New Hampshire State Constitution</a> of 1783 is the supreme law of the state, followed by the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_Revised_Statutes_Annotated" title="New Hampshire Revised Statutes Annotated">New Hampshire Revised Statutes Annotated</a> and the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_Code_of_Administrative_Rules" title="New Hampshire Code of Administrative Rules">New Hampshire Code of Administrative Rules</a>. These are roughly analogous to the federal <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Code" title="United States Code">United States Code</a> and <a href="/wiki/Code_of_Federal_Regulations" title="Code of Federal Regulations">Code of Federal Regulations</a> respectively.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Branches_of_government">Branches of government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Hampshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Branches of government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>New Hampshire has a bifurcated executive branch, consisting of the governor and a five-member <a href="/wiki/Executive_Council_of_New_Hampshire" title="Executive Council of New Hampshire">executive council</a> which votes on state contracts worth more than $5,000 and "advises and consents" to the governor's nominations to major state positions such as department heads and all judgeships and <a href="/wiki/Pardon" title="Pardon">pardon</a> requests. New Hampshire does not have a <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_governor_(United_States)#New_Hampshire" title="Lieutenant governor (United States)">lieutenant governor</a>; the Senate president serves as "acting governor" whenever the governor is unable to perform the duties.</p>
<p>The legislature is called the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_General_Court" title="New Hampshire General Court">General Court</a>. It consists of the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_House_of_Representatives" title="New Hampshire House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a> and the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_Senate" title="New Hampshire Senate">Senate</a>. There are 400 representatives, making it one of the largest elected bodies in the English-speaking world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> and 24 senators. Most are effectively volunteers, nearly half of which are <a href="/wiki/Retirement" title="Retirement">retirees</a>. (For details, see the article on <a href="/wiki/Government_of_New_Hampshire#Legislative_Branch" title="Government of New Hampshire">Government of New Hampshire</a>.)</p>
<p>The state's sole appellate court is the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_Supreme_Court" title="New Hampshire Supreme Court">New Hampshire Supreme Court</a>. The <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_Superior_Court" title="New Hampshire Superior Court">Superior Court</a> is the court of general jurisdiction and the only court which provides for jury trials in <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(common_law)" title="Civil law (common law)">civil</a> or <a href="/wiki/Criminal_law" title="Criminal law">criminal</a> cases. The other state courts are the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_Probate_Court" title="New Hampshire Probate Court">Probate Court</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_District_Court" title="New Hampshire District Court">District Court</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_Family_Division" title="New Hampshire Family Division">Family Division</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_government">Local government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Hampshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Local government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</td>

Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small><i>(April 2013)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>New Hampshire has <a href="/wiki/List_of_counties_in_New_Hampshire" title="List of counties in New Hampshire">10 counties</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_and_towns_in_New_Hampshire" title="List of cities and towns in New Hampshire">234 cities and towns</a>.</p>
<p>New Hampshire is a <a href="/wiki/John_Forrest_Dillon" title="John Forrest Dillon">"Dillon Rule"</a> state, meaning that the state retains all powers not specifically granted to municipalities. Even so, the legislature strongly favors local control, particularly with regard to land use regulations. New Hampshire municipalities are classified as <a href="/wiki/New_England_town" title="New England town">towns</a> or cities, which differ primarily by the form of government. Most towns generally operate on the <a href="/wiki/Town_meeting" title="Town meeting">town meeting</a> form of government, where the registered voters in the town act as the town legislature, and a <a href="/wiki/Board_of_selectmen" title="Board of selectmen">board of selectmen</a> acts as the executive of the town. Larger towns and the state's thirteen cities operate either on a <a href="/wiki/Council%E2%80%93manager_government" title="Council–manager government">council-manager</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mayor%E2%80%93council_government" title="Mayor–council government">council-mayor</a> form of government. There is no difference, from the point of view of the state government, between towns and cities besides the form of government. All state-level statutes treat all municipalities identically.</p>
<p>New Hampshire has a small number of <a href="/wiki/Unincorporated_area" title="Unincorporated area">unincorporated areas</a> that are titled as grants, locations, purchases, or townships. These locations have limited to no self-government, and services are generally provided for them by neighboring towns or the county or state where needed. As of the 2000 census, there were 25 of these left in New Hampshire, accounting for a total population of 173 people (as of 2000<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=New_Hampshire&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>); several were entirely depopulated. All but two of these unincorporated areas are located in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coos_County,_New_Hampshire" title="Coos County, New Hampshire">Coos County</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Hampshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_New_Hampshire" title="Politics of New Hampshire">Politics of New Hampshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Political_party_strength_in_New_Hampshire" title="Political party strength in New Hampshire">Political party strength in New Hampshire</a></div>
